tool
1	"""PyTorch reference for a Kimi-Linear W4A16 hybrid decode unit (batch=1).
2	
3	Correctness oracle, NOT the fast baseline (baseline.py) or the SOTA ceiling.
4	One repeating motif of Kimi-Linear-48B-A3B -- 3 KDA (gated-delta linear attn)
5	layers + 1 MLA (multi-head latent attention) layer, each + a 64-expert MoE FFN
6	-- decoded one token at a time, with the big projection weights stored as
7	**W4A16** (int4 weights, group-128 asymmetric, bf16 accumulation; the AWQ/GPTQ
8	format that open-source inference actually ships).
9	
10	Why W4A16 and not fp8: at batch-1 decode the bottleneck is *weight memory
11	traffic*. int4 weights are 0.25x the bytes of bf16 (4x less traffic), and a
12	fused dequant-GEMV realizes that win in the memory-bound regime where decode
… +360 more lines

[Read] /ephemeral/mega-outputs/runs/20260716_024329_kinetic-claude_kinetic-0715_02_kimi_linear_decode/repo/problems/02_kimi_linear_decode/baseline.py
tool
1	"""Optimized-PyTorch W4A16 baseline for the Kimi-Linear decode unit.
2	
3	The floor the solution must beat -- NOT the oracle (reference.py). Same int4
4	weights, same math, written the competent pure-PyTorch way: bf16 accumulation
5	and batched MoE expert dequant instead of the reference's fp32 per-expert loop.
6	
7	It still *materializes* each dequantized bf16 weight before the matmul (int4
8	read + bf16 write + bf16 read = ~9x the traffic of a fused path). That is the
9	whole point left on the table: stock PyTorch cannot fuse the int4 unpack into
10	the GEMV, so beating this baseline requires a custom fused dequant-GEMV kernel.
11	`score = baseline_latency / solution_latency`.
12	"""

tool
1	"""Correctness runner for the Kimi-Linear hybrid decode unit.
2	
3	Per-step correctness, not a chained trajectory: for each seed and a couple of
4	context lengths, the reference and the solution are given an IDENTICAL fed
5	state and one new token, each runs ONE decode step, and we compare the
6	next-token hidden plus the updated decode state (KDA recurrent state, MLA latent
7	cache) by cosine similarity. Cosine sim is robust to bf16/fp8 precision but
8	still rejects wrong answers. Chaining steps would just measure how fast two
9	precisions diverge, which is not a kernel-correctness signal.
10	"""
11	from __future__ import annotations

But there's the well-known "absorb" trick: instead of computing k_nope = c_kv @ W_kvb, compute scores as q_nope·(c_kv@W_k) = (q_nope @ W_k^T)·c_kv. Since kv_b is a weight (fixed), we can pre-absorb: scores = (q_nope @ W_UK^T) @ c_kv^T + q_rope @ k_rope^T. And o = p @ v = p @ (c_kv @ W_UV) = (p @ c_kv) @ W_UV. Must be single kernel launch. Single kernel approach: one big Triton kernel with a grid covering all work, using program_id to select which GEMV/operation to do. With sequential dependencies between operations (rmsnorm → gemv → conv → state update → gemv ...), we need global synchronization between stages inside one kernel launch. Options: 1. Grid-wide sync via cooperative groups (CUDA) — tricky in Triton; Triton has no grid sync except via `tl.extra` or hacks. Actually there's no portable grid.sync() in Triton. However, there IS a persistent-kernel approach: launch a fixed number of CTAs (e.g., = #SMs = 188 on this GPU? RTX PRO 6000 has 188 SMs? Let me check — RTX PRO 6000 Blackwell = GB202, 188 SMs? Actually RTX 6000 PRO has 188 SMs (24064 CUDA cores / 128 = 188). Then use atomic-counter-based software barriers between stages within the persistent kernel. 2. Do the layering with atomics: each program works on stage i, waits until a global counter signals that prerequisite stage finished. Software global barrier = each block atomicAdd on arrival, spin-wait until counter == numBlocks. This works but spin loops in Triton are possible (while loop with atomic load). It's been done (e.g., persistent megakernels). 3. Alternatively, structure the whole step as a sequence of "phases" where each phase is embarrassingly parallel, and use one kernel launch where each CTA iterates over phases with grid sync between them. Alternative approach: avoid needing global sync where dependencies are local. But the RMSNorm after a GEMV over 2304 dims needs the full hidden vector — global dependency. I think software grid barrier persistent kernel is the way. Triton: I can do barrier with tl.atomic_add on a global int32 counter and spin with `while tl.atomic_add(...)`? With modest register/smem use, we can fit e.g. 2-4 CTAs/SM. Realistically ~1.5-3µs per barrier. 40 barriers = 60-120µs. Not great. Reduce number of phases: can we fuse phases so we need fewer barriers? Note RMSNorm needs full vector. GEMV: rows partitioned across CTAs, next stage consumes all → barrier. The state S is 32×128×128 fp32 = 2MB, in global memory. Barrier implementation in Triton: ``` # barrier: each CTA bumps counter with release semantics, last CTA flips flag tl.atomic_add(cnt_ptr, 1, sem='acq_rel') # arrival if old == N-1: tl.atomic_xchg(flag_ptr, phase, sem='release')? while tl.atomic_add(flag_ptr, 0, sem='acquire') < phase: pass ``` Triton atomic sem options available: 'acquire','release','acq_rel','relaxed'. Spin loop in Triton: use a while loop: ``` phase_done = False while not phase_done: v = tl.atomic_add(flag, 0, sem="acquire") phase_done = v >= target ``` Hmm, Triton supports while loops via `while` in recent versions (tl.while_loop? reasoning
Baseline: 7.6-9.1 ms/token. That's slower than my 1.1ms estimate — probably because dequant + einsum materialization overhead plus Python overhead of many small ops per step (the MoE loop and einsums). Also at batch-1 the einsums are GEMVs; dequantizing 8 experts ×3 matrices of (2304,1024) each = lots of traffic. Anyway, baseline ~7.6-9.1ms. If I hit ~180-250µs, speedupundefined ≈ 30-45x. Excellent. Even 500µs would be ~16x. Now the Python overhead concern: baseline is heavily Python/launch-overhead bound? 131 tok/s at ctx2048 vs 110 at 16384 — the ctx length barely matters (only MLA cache), so most of the 7.6ms is fixed overhead: hundreds of kernel launches per step. Config numbers (fixed): - d = 2304, group = 128 → ng = 18 groups for d=2304 - H_kda = 32, Dk = 128 → C = 4096 - pattern ("K","K","K","M") - MLA: H=32, kv_lora 512, qk_nope 128, qk_rope 64, v_head 128 → q_proj out = 32*(128+64) = 6144; kv_a out = 576; kv_b: in 512, out = 32*(128+128) = 8192; o_proj: in 4096, out 2304 - MoE: E=64, active 8, shared 1, inter 1024, routed_scaling 2.446 - rope theta 10000 Weight streams per block: - KDA: q,k,v,g: in 2304 out 4096 ×4 (packed 2304/2×4096 = 4.72MB each); o_proj: in 4096 out 2304 (4.72MB) - MoE per block: 9 slots × [gate 2304→1024 (1.18MB), up same, down 1024→2304 (1.18MBλ)] Now let me define the kernel's param structure. All buffers passed via a device pointer array + scalars struct. I'll pass a single struct containing: ```c struct Params { // weights per block/layer: 4 blocks; each either KDA or MLA + MoE // We flatten: for each block b<4: // kind // attn_norm ptr (bf16 [2304]), moe_norm ptr // KDA: wq,wk,wv,wg,wo (uint8*), sq,sk,sv,sg,so, zq,zk,zv,zg,zo (bf16) // conv_w (bf16 [3,4096,4]), beta_w (bf16 [32,2304]) // nn.Linear weight [out,in] = [32,2304] // MLA: q,kv_a,kv_b,o_proj weights (uint8* + scales + zeros) // MoE: router_w (bf16 [64,2304]), gate/up/down w_q uint8 [64][...], scales, zeros; shared gate/up/down // scalars: L (current context len, = position of new token), gen base, ntasks per phase... } #define NPHASE 23 struct PhaseInfo { int ntasks; ... } ``` Building this every step in Python = costly-ish. Alternative: build the param struct ONCE (as a CPU bytes buffer pinned), cache the device copy; per step only update scalars (L, x_in ptr, step gen base). Python keeps ~(weights list) referencing tensors; data_ptr() cached on first step. Kernel reads params from GLOBAL memory (a struct in device mem). ~120 pointers × 8B = 1KB. Loaded by threads into... each thread only needs a few. Fine to just read from global directly where used (hot in L1 after first read). Actually — simpler and less error-prone: pass params via __constant__? 64KB limit, need cudaMemcpyToSymbol per step (~1KB H2D, fine) but with load_inline the symbol handling is fine. Or kernel arg struct up to 32KB (CUDA 12.1+): pass the whole struct BY VALUE as kernel arg, updated per step at CUDA driver level (no memcpy needed). I'll use a by-value struct (static size ~1-2KB). Kernel arg passing with cooperative launch: cudaLaunchCooperativeKernel takes void** args → struct by value works. Grid config: dim3 blocks(sms), 1024 threads. Counters (task grab + barrier gen): device global int array, persistent buffer (allocated at init), zeroed initially; barrier gen monotonic; task ctrs reset in epilogue. +"""Kimi-Linear W4A16 hybrid decode unit -- single-megakernel solution.
+
+The entire per-token decode step (3x KDA + 1x MLA attention, 4x MoE FFN,
+all int4 dequant GEMVs, convs, state updates, RMSNorms and residuals) runs
+as ONE CUDA kernel launch inside step(). Grid-wide phase barriers are done
+with a custom atomic barrier inside a cooperatively-launched persistent
+kernel (188 blocks x 1024 threads on the RTX PRO 6000).
+
+W4A16 dequant is fused into every GEMV: int4 nibbles are unpacked and
+scaled per-128-group on the fly; no bf16 weight matrix is ever written out.
+
+KIMI_EAGER=1 forces the slow-but-faithful eager path (debugging aid).
+"""

Task-grab helper: ``` DEVI int grab(unsigned* ctrslot, int ntasks, int& stask_sh){ __syncthreads(); if (threadIdx.x == 0) stask_sh = atomicAdd(ctrslot, 1u); __syncthreads(); return stask_sh; } ``` Loop: while(true){ t = grab(...); if (t >= ntasks) break; ... } Wait, careful with syncthreads inside: after `break`, other threads... whole block breaks together (stask_sh uniform). ok. gbar: ``` DEVI void gbar(unsigned* cnt, unsigned* gen, unsigned target){ __syncthreads(); if (threadIdx.x == 0){ __threadfence(); unsigned a = atomicAdd(cnt, 1u); if (a == gridDim.x - 1){ atomicExch(cnt, 0u); __threadfence(); atomicAdd(gen, 1u); } while (atomicAdd(gen, 0u) < target) __nanosleep(32); } __syncthreads(); } ``` Main kernel: ``` extern "C" __global__ void __launch_bounds__(1024,1) kimi_mega(const long long* __restrict__ SP, DynArgs dyn){ extern __shared__ char smem[]; unsigned* ctr = (unsigned*)SP[kCTR]; unsigned* gen = (unsigned*)SP[kGEN]; unsigned target = dyn.gen_base; int slot = 0; for (int b = 0; b < 4; ++b){ if (dyn.kind[b] == 0){ if (slot <= dyn.stop_after) phase_k1(SP, dyn, b, smem, ctr, slot); barrier: gbar(cnt, gen, ++target); slot++; ... k2, k3, k4, k5 } else { ... m1..m7 } } if (slot <= dyn.stop_after) phase_epi(...); } ``` Wait gen_base/target: target incremented per barrier: first barrier target = gen_base + 1. gbar(cnt, gen, ++target): starts at gen_base+1. host: gen_base += nbarriers = nslots-1... nslots includes epilogue: barriers after each slot except after epilogue: nbarriers = nslots - 1 where nslots = 4-block slots + 1. +"""Megakernel runner for the Kimi-Linear W4A16 decode unit.
+
+Builds one CUDA kernel (via torch.utils.cpp_extension.load_inline) that
+executes the ENTIRE per-token decode step as a single cooperative launch:
+3x [KDA attn + MoE] + 1x [MLA attn + MoE], all int4 dequant GEMVs fused.
+Phase ordering inside the kernel is enforced by a custom atomic grid
+barrier (the kernel is launched cooperatively, so all blocks are resident).
+"""
+from __future__ import annotations
+
+import os
+
+import torch

Mma fragment layouts cribbed from memory (mma.m16n8k16 f32.bf16.bf16.f32): - A frag (16×16 bf16): each lane 4 regs (8 halves): a0..a3: row = lane/4 + {0,8}: col pairs = (lane%4)*2 + {0,1, +8}: standard: a0: (groupID, threadID_in_group*2 + {0,1}) = m-row lane>>2, k-col (lane&3)*2 pairs [0,1] a1: row + 8, same cols... mapping: fragment index: {a0: row r=lane/4, cols c= (lane%4)*2, +1}, {a1: r+8, same c}, {a2: r, c+8..}, {a3: r+8, c+8..} — quad layout. - B frag (16×8 bf16): 2 regs (4 halves): row = k-dim: b0: k = lane%4*2? B col-major: b0: col = lane/4 rows: B[k, n]: lane holds: n = lane/4? ... col j = lane>>2, k = (lane&3)*2 + {0,1}, second reg: k+8? hmm — standard B frag: {b0: k=(lane&3)*2+{0,1}, n=lane>>2}... - C frag (16×8 fp32): 4 regs: c0: (row r=lane>>2, col (lane&3)*2+0,1): c0,c1 = row r cols 2(lane&3)+{0,1}; c2,c3 = row r+8 same cols. Getting these right from memory is risky but the PTX doc patterns are standard. To avoid layout bugs, DON'T hand-extract C for softmax per element with wrong mapping — instead write C frags to smem via the SAME mapping (c0 row r...) using the documented mapping, then softmax on smem rows [h, n] with linear indexing — safe!! Then P written to smem, and mma2 A-frags loaded via ldmatrix (which handles A layout by definition). For B frags, use ldmatrix.trans which just gives you the fragment hardware expects (no manual mapping) ✓.
